The House of Lost Mirrors

Chapter 1

CHAPTER ONE

Havana’s bones were sinking into the sea. From his tower, the architect watched saltwater creep through the cobbled plazas he’d designed for a future that never arrived. The city’s new skin—a sheath of polished chrome and weeping rust—grew over the old, a monstrous graft. His masterpiece was being consumed by its own blueprint.

My name is Leo Varga. I find things that are not meant to be found. Not missing persons, not stolen trinkets—those are for the police and the desperate. I find echoes. Traces. The after-images of events that have burned themselves into the fabric of a place. A client once called me a “resonance detective,” and the term, pretentious as it is, has stuck. I listen to rooms. I read the silence in attics. I follow stains that are not quite visible. My office is my notebook; my tools are patience and a certain permeable quality of mind that most people would call a liability.

The ghost I was waiting for was named Klara. She had found me through a chain of whispers, the kind that travel through the back rooms of antiquarian bookshops and the parlours of certain discreet psychiatrists. Her letter was on thick, cream-laid paper, the handwriting a sharp, vertical script that spoke of old schools and older money.

Herr Varga, it began, I require a consultation regarding my brother, Emil. He is not missing. He is, by all accounts, entirely present. But he is not my brother. Something has been exchanged in the night. I reside at Haus der Verlorenen Spiegel, Canovagasse 9. Please come after dark.

The Haus der Verlorenen Spiegel. The House of Lost Mirrors. A name like a fairy tale, or a warning. Canovagasse was a seam in the city’s fabric, a narrow lane in the Alsergrund district where the grand 19th-century apartment blocks seemed to lean closer together, sharing secrets. Number 9 was no different: a soot-stained façade, high windows that reflected nothing but the building opposite, a heavy oak door with a brass knocker shaped like a satyr’s head, its mouth agape in a silent scream.

I raised the knocker and let it fall. The sound was swallowed by the wood, by the rain, by the street. A moment passed, then two. I was about to lift it again when the door swung inward, not with a creak, but with a soft, oiled sigh.

She stood in the dim foyer, backlit by the weak glow of a single, crystal-chandeliered bulb. Klara. She was perhaps fifty, but carried herself with a tension that suggested a perpetual, coiled alertness. Her hair, the colour of cold ash, was swept back severely. She wore a high-necked dress of charcoal-grey wool, unadorned. Her eyes were the most striking feature: a pale, clear grey, like quartz, and they assessed me with an unnerving, total focus.

“Herr Varga. You are punctual. Come in. Leave the weather at the door.”

Her voice was low, precise, each word placed like a stone in a mosaic. I stepped over the threshold. The air inside was several degrees colder than the street, and carried a scent of beeswax, old paper, and something else—a faint, metallic tang, like ozone after a lightning strike.

The foyer was a vault of shadows. The walls were panelled in dark wood, and they were lined, floor to ceiling, with mirrors. Or rather, with empty mirror frames. Dozens of them, in every shape and size: ornate Baroque ovals with gilded grapevines, severe Art Nouveau rectangles, simple circles of bevelled glass. Each frame held not a reflection, but a square of black velvet, neatly pinned in place. It was like being stared at by a crowd of blind eyes.

“A collection,” I said.

“An inheritance,” she corrected, closing the door. The sound of the rain vanished, replaced by a dense, listening silence. “My family’s. They do not reflect. They absorb. This way.”

She led me through an archway into a drawing room. Here, the oppressive décor gave way to something sparer, but no less tense. A Biedermeier sofa, two armchairs upholstered in faded damask, a grand piano with its lid shut like a sealed coffin. A fire smouldered in a green-tiled Kachelofen, its heat not reaching far. And there, standing before a bookcase, was the brother.

Emil turned as we entered. He was a man in his early fifties, with thinning blond hair and a soft, scholarly face. He wore a cardigan and corduroy trousers. He smiled. It was a perfect, pleasant, empty smile.

“Ah, the investigator. Klara mentioned you might call. Can I offer you a schnapps? It’s a wretched night.”

His voice was amiable, his demeanour relaxed. He moved to a sideboard and poured two glasses of clear liquid from a cut-crystal decanter. His movements were fluid, unremarkable.

Klara did not sit. She stood by the fireplace, her quartz eyes fixed on him. “Tell him, Emil. Tell him about the manuscript.”

Emil handed me a glass. Our fingers did not touch. “My sister is obsessed with my work. I’m translating a rather obscure medieval text, a bestiary of sorts from the St. Florian monastery. Fascinating stuff, really. Allegories of sin, descriptions of beasts that never were… or perhaps were. It consumes my days. Klara seems to think it consumes me.”

“It is not the work,” Klara said, her voice a wire. “It is the change. The… substitution.”

Emil chuckled, a dry, papery sound. “She speaks in metaphors, Herr Varga. Always has. Since we were children. I’ve been engrossed, I admit. A little absent-minded. Forgetting dinners, losing track of conversations. The curse of the academic.” He took a sip of his schnapps. “But ‘substitution’? Really, Klara. It’s melodramatic.”

I tasted my drink. It was Zirbenz, piney and sharp. I let my gaze wander the room, not looking at anything directly, letting the periphery speak. The room was orderly, fastidiously so. But there were dissonances. A thin layer of dust on the piano lid, but none on the bookshelves. The fire, too small for the chill. And Emil himself. He was plausible. He was perfectly plausible. That was the problem.

“The text,” I said. “This bestiary. Where do you work on it?”

“In the study. Top of the house. Would you like to see?”

“Yes.”

Klara’s gaze met mine. There was a flicker in it—something like relief, or dread.

We ascended a broad, carpeted staircase, past landings hung with more blind mirrors. The house seemed to tighten around us, the air growing thinner, colder. The top floor was a converted attic space, a long room with a sloping ceiling braced by dark beams. A massive oak desk stood under a dormer window, covered in a chaos of papers, open books, and notecards. A green-shaded banker’s lamp cast a pool of jaundiced light. The walls here were not panelled; they were plaster, and they were covered in pencil sketches. Not of mythical beasts, but of intricate, geometric patterns—interlocking circles, labyrinthine spirals, shapes that seemed to fold in on themselves. They were drawn with a frantic, obsessive precision.

“My doodles,” Emil said, waving a dismissive hand. “The mind wanders while translating.”

I moved to the desk. The central manuscript was a facsimile, pages of vellum covered in dense, Gothic script, illuminated with bizarre, vibrant miniatures. I saw a bird with a human face, a tree whose roots were serpents, a knight fighting a creature that was all mouth and teeth. But beside it, on modern paper, was Emil’s translation. I leaned closer.

The English was fluid, scholarly. But the words… they did not describe. They invoked. They were not about the beasts; they seemed to be instructions, or incantations. Phrases repeated, mutated. “The skin that is not a skin… the breath that is not air… the memory that feeds on the rememberer…”

I looked up at a line of text in the facsimile, then at Emil’s translation notecard for that line. They did not match. Not even close. The Latin phrase “corpus illusorium” was rendered not as “illusory body,” but as “the borrowed flesh.”

“Your translation is… interpretive,” I said.

Emil’s pleasant smile didn’t waver. “All translation is interpretation, Herr Varga. The medieval mind did not think in our categories. One must find equivalencies in the spirit, not just the letter.”

A chill that had nothing to do with the attic air traced my spine. I turned from the desk and examined the geometric drawings on the wall. Up close, their complexity was dizzying. They were not random. They were schematics. Blueprints. For what, I couldn’t say.

“And the mirrors?” I asked, turning back to him. “The frames downstairs. Why cover them?”

For the first time, a crack appeared in his placidity. A tiny tic at the corner of his left eye. “A family eccentricity. My grandfather started it. He believed mirrors could trap things. Better to keep them… quiet.”

Klara, who had been silent in the doorway, spoke. “Emil used to look in mirrors. He was vain about his hair. Now, he does not glance at them. He walks past them as if they are walls.”

“A sign of maturity, sister dear,” Emil said, but his voice had hardened, just a fraction.

I finished the schnapps, the pine taste now cloying. “I’ll need to spend some time in the house. To listen.”

“Listen?” Emil asked.

“To the silence. It often has more to say than the people in it.”

Klara nodded. “You may have the run of it. Except for Emil’s study at night. He works late. He must not be disturbed.”

“Of course,” I said.

Emil’s smile returned, full force. “Then by all means, listen away, Herr Varga. I’m sure you’ll find nothing but the sighs of an old house and the ramblings of an old woman.” He said it lightly, but the words hung in the cold air, deliberate and cruel.

Klara did not flinch. She simply turned and walked back down the stairs. I followed, feeling Emil’s quartz-like gaze on my back until I rounded the landing.

Back in the drawing room, Klara stood by the blind mirror over the fireplace. “You see,” she said, not a question.

“I see a man translating a dangerous text and a sister worried for him,” I said, carefully.

“No.” She turned to me. “You saw what I see. The wrongness. It is in the details. The way he butters his bread now—from the centre out, in a perfect spiral. He never did that. He hates pears. He ate one yesterday, core and all. He sleeps, but the bed is cold in the morning. He breathes, but on the window glass, in the cold study, his breath does not fog.” Her voice dropped to a whisper. “He is a perfect copy. And the copy is learning, but it has not mastered the details. Not all of them.”

The rain tapped against the windows. The fire popped. I believed her. Not because of the pears or the breath, but because of the feeling in the attic. The frantic, geometric hunger on those walls. The translation that was not a translation.

“I will stay tonight,” I said. “In the house.”

“There is a guest room on the second floor. It is… undisturbed.”

She showed me to a room that was a museum piece: a four-poster bed with heavy curtains, a washstand with a porcelain pitcher, a rug so thin it was barely a memory of wool. Another blind mirror hung opposite the bed. I set my small valise down.

“What happened the night you noticed the change?” I asked.

She paused at the door. “There was a storm. Not of rain. Of silence. It was so absolute it woke me. I came to the hall. The door to his study at the top of the house was open. A light was on. I saw him standing at the threshold, silhouetted. He was not moving. He was… listening. To the silence. And then he turned his head. Not his body. Just his head. Further than a head should turn. He looked down the stairs at me. And he smiled. That is when I knew my brother was gone.”

She closed the door softly.

I sat on the edge of the bed. The house settled around me, a symphony of tiny creaks and pops. I opened my valise and took out my notebook, a pencil, a small, brass candleholder with a black candle. I do not use modern recording devices. They capture sound, not resonance.

I lit the candle. The flame drew a small, warm circle of light in the vast, cold room. I blew out the lamp. I sat in the chair by the window, watching the rain-streaked glass, and I began to listen.

I listened past the patter of rain, past the groan of a pipe. I listened for the shape of the silence Klara had described. It took an hour. Then, I heard it.

From above. A sound that was not a sound. A vibration, so low it was felt in the teeth, in the marrow. A rhythmic, grinding hum, like a great machine turning over beneath the earth. It pulsed through the floorboards, up the legs of the chair. It was coming from the attic study.

And then, a new sound. Sharp, clear. The sound of a pencil snapping.

I stood, silent. I moved to the door, opened it. The hallway was a tunnel of darkness. The hum was clearer here, a sub-aural threnody. I walked to the base of the stairs leading to the attic. The door at the top was shut. A line of yellow light showed beneath it.

I climbed. The hum grew. It was not mechanical. It was organic. A chanting of stone, a melody of dust. I reached the top. I did not touch the door. I knelt and put my eye to the keyhole.

The study was awash in the green lamplight. Emil was not at the desk. He stood in the centre of the room, before a large, clear space on the plaster wall. He had a fresh pencil in his hand. He was drawing, not on paper, but directly on the wall. His arm moved with a swift, inhuman certainty, tracing one of the complex geometric patterns. But this was no sketch. As he drew, the lines glowed with a faint, phosphorescent blue light, burning into the plaster. The hum was emanating from the pattern itself.

He was not just drawing it. He was opening it.

He finished a segment, a series of interlocking spirals, and stepped back. The pattern on the wall pulsed, light flowing through its channels like blood through a circulatory system. The air in the room thickened, warped. The light from the lamp bent around something that was beginning to bulge from the wall, from the centre of the design.

A shape. Indistinct, shimmering like a heat haze. It had weight. It pressed against the surface of our world from somewhere else.

Emil watched, his head cocked, his expression one of avid, clinical curiosity. The pleasant smile was gone. In its place was a look of pure, hungry observation.

The thing in the wall coalesced further. For a second, I saw the suggestion of limbs, of a elongated head, of eyes that were not eyes but voids. It reached a tendril of distorted air towards Emil.

He spoke, his voice not his own—a dry rustle, like pages turning in a tomb. “Not yet. The bridge is not complete. Soon.”

He lifted the pencil again and drew a single, swift line through the centre of the pattern. The blue light flared, then died. The hum ceased, cut off as if by a guillotine. The shape in the wall dissipated, the wall becoming mere plaster and pencil marks again.

Emil dropped the pencil. He straightened his cardigan. He walked to the desk, sat down, and picked up a notecard, as if reviewing a day’s work. The scholar again. The copy.

I crept back down the stairs, my heart a cold, hard stone in my chest. Klara was not mad. Something had been exchanged in the night. And it was not finished. It was building a door.

Back in my room, I looked at the blind mirror across from my bed. The black velvet seemed to drink the candlelight. I thought of Klara’s words: They do not reflect. They absorb.

What, I wondered, had they absorbed before they were covered? And what was now trying to get out?

I blew out the candle. In the sudden, total dark, the memory of that low hum seemed to vibrate in my bones. I did not sleep. I listened. And the house, the House of Lost Mirrors, listened back.

# Chapter 2
## CHAPTER TWO
The grey light of a Vienna dawn did not so much illuminate the room as dilute the darkness. It was a slow, grudging process. I had spent the night in the chair, watching the line under the door, listening for footsteps on the stairs that never came. The hum had not returned. The house had settled into a watchful, exhausted silence, like a predator after a meal.
At seven, I heard movement elsewhere—the distant clink of china, the muffled tread of feet. A domestic rhythm, painfully normal. I splashed icy water from the pitcher on my face, the shock clearing the residue of dread from my mind. In the mirror over the washstand—a small, functional one, thankfully uncovered—my reflection looked back, hollow-eyed, stubbled. I focused on the mundane ritual of shaving, the scrape of the blade a grounding, physical fact.
Downstairs, the dining room was a long, narrow cell with a single window overlooking a confined courtyard. Klara sat at the head of a mahogany table, a cup of black coffee before her. She wore a different dress, identical in severity. She did not look as if she had slept.
“Herr Varga. Did you listen?”
“I did.” I took the seat she indicated. “He was in the study after midnight. He was drawing on the wall. The patterns glow.”
A flicker in her quartz eyes. Not surprise, but confirmation. “The bridge,” she whispered.
“He called it that. A bridge. Something was trying to come through. Something he told to wait.”
She closed her eyes for a long moment. When she opened them, they were dry, hard. “Then we must un-build it. Before it is complete.”
“We need to understand what we’re dealing with. The original text. Your family history. The mirrors. Everything.”
Before she could answer, Emil entered. He moved with a buoyant step, humming a fragment of a Strauss waltz. He wore a tweed jacket today, a silk cravat at his throat. The consummate gentleman scholar.
“Good morning! I trust you slept well, Herr Varga? These old beds can be like stone quarries.” He served himself from a sideboard of silver-domed dishes—eggs, sausage, rolls. His movements were efficient, graceful. He sat opposite me and began to eat with precise, measured bites. “Klara, you’re not eating. You’ll waste away.”
“I have no appetite,” she said, her voice flat.
“A pity.” He turned his pleasant, empty smile on me. “And what is your professional opinion, Herr Varga? Have you diagnosed our familial malaise?”
“I’m still taking notes,” I said, stirring my coffee. “The manuscript is fascinating. The Liber de Figuris Invisibilibus. I looked up the title. A very rare text. Suppressed, I believe.”
His chewing slowed for a fraction of a second. “You are well-informed. Yes, the Church found its… diagrams… troubling. It posits a geometry of realities, you see. Not a spiritual cosmology, but a literal, spatial one. Adjacent dimensions, folded into our own. The patterns are maps. Keys.”
“And the beasts it describes? The illusorium corpus?”
“Metaphors,” he said, waving a fork. “For the entities that might inhabit those adjacent spaces. Thought-forms. Psychological projections given temporary coherence by the correct… architectural formulae.”
He was so smooth, so reasonable. He was giving me the academic cover story, and it was plausible. It was what the real Emil might have said. The copy was learning, refining its performance.
“And the mirrors?” I pressed. “Your grandfather’s eccentricity. Would he have seen these ‘thought-forms’?”
Emil’s smile tightened at the edges. “My grandfather was a troubled man. He believed he saw things in reflective surfaces. Things that should not be there. He covered them to preserve his sanity. And ours.” He took a sip of coffee. “Superstition, of course. But sometimes superstition is a useful quarantine.”
The conversation continued, a delicate fencing match over breakfast. He parried every probe with erudition and charm. Klara said nothing, a silent statue of judgement at the table’s head.
After breakfast, Emil announced he had correspondence to attend to and retired to his study. The moment the door closed, Klara stood.
“Come. There is something you must see. Before he decides to hide it.”
She led me not upstairs, but down. A door beneath the main staircase opened onto a steep, narrow servants’ stairway, leading to the cellar. The air grew damp and cold, smelling of earth and coal dust. At the bottom, she flicked a switch. A single, bare bulb flickered on, revealing a cavernous space with a vaulted brick ceiling. It was not used for storage. It was a workshop.
A long, heavy table ran down the centre, scarred and stained. Tools hung on the walls—not hammers and saws, but delicate, sinister things: glass-cutters, glazier’s diamonds, frames of lead came, pots of dark, tarry cement. And on the table, in various states of completion, were mirrors. Not the ornate antiques upstairs, but new ones. Their glass was not clear, but had a smoky, depthless quality, like obsidian.
“He makes them,” Klara said, her voice echoing in the vault. “At night, sometimes. I hear him down here. The grinding, the cutting. He never did this before.”
I approached the table. One mirror was nearly finished, its glass set into a simple, modern frame of brushed steel. My reflection in its surface was not my reflection. It was a smudged, grey silhouette, featureless. It moved when I moved, but sluggishly, a half-second behind, like a shadow in thick water.
“What is this glass?”
“I do not know. He orders it from a foundry in Bohemia. Special order. He calls it ‘absorbent substrate.’” She went to a corner, pulled a dustsheet from a small, locked cabinet. With a key from her pocket, she opened it. Inside were not tools, but notebooks. Dozens of them, leather-bound, their pages thick with handwriting. “My grandfather’s journals. And my father’s. Emil never cared for them. He called them the ravings of melancholics. But now… I think he reads them. He comes down here.”
I took the top journal. The handwriting was a frantic, spidery scrawl. The date on the first page was 1898.
October 12. The reflection in the dressing mirror did not follow me today. It stood still, watching me dress. When I turned, it was gone. The surface was cold to the touch.
November 3. Covered the large mirror in the hall. The velvet seems to quiet the whispers. For now.
January 17. It is not a reflection. It is a tenant. It lives in the silver behind the glass. It learns our shapes, our habits. It grows hungry for the warmth on this side.
I looked up. Klara was watching me, her arms wrapped around herself against the cellar chill. “He saw them. All of them did. It is a family inheritance, Herr Varga. Not the house. The sight. The sensitivity to what lingers in the thresholds. Emil had it, too, as a boy. He would scream at his reflection. Then he learned to ignore it. To bury it in books. Until that manuscript dug it up again.”
“And the covered mirrors upstairs…?”
“Are not empty. The velvet is a seal. A quarantine, as Emil said. But not against superstition. Against what is trapped inside.”
The pieces were aligning, forming a picture more terrifying than a simple haunting. This was taxonomy. Engineering. “The manuscript gave him the theory,” I said. “The geometry. Your family’s journals gave him the practical history. The mirrors are not just prisons. They are… lenses. Or doors. He’s not just letting something in. He’s trying to create a permanent passage. A bridge made of glass and stolen reflection.”
The sound of a floorboard creaking directly above our heads made us both freeze. We looked up at the cellar ceiling. Slow, deliberate footsteps crossed the floor of the hall above. They paused directly over the cellar door.
Klara’s hand found my arm, her grip like iron. We stood in the weak bulb’s light, not breathing. The footsteps moved on, fading towards the back of the house.
“We cannot stay here,” she breathed.
“We have to,” I said, though the same instinct screamed in me. “We need evidence. Something tangible. The journal, the mirrors he’s making… it’s all circumstantial. We need to see the process. We need to know what his end is.”
“His end is my brother’s body, walking around, speaking with his voice!” Her whisper was fierce, frayed. “What more evidence do you need?”
“Evidence for whom, Klara? If I go to the authorities, they will see a nervous woman and a reclusive academic. We need to break the bridge. To do that, we need to understand its keystone.”
She released my arm, deflating. She knew I was right. “What do you propose?”
“Tonight. I need to get into the study while he is there. Not to watch through a keyhole. To see the other side of the page.”

The day passed with a torturous, surface normality. I walked the district, trying to clear my head. The rain had stopped, leaving the streets slick and gleaming. I found the address of the Bohemian glassworks in an old directory at a public library. I sent a brief, coded telegram to an old contact in Prague, a historian of obscure texts, asking for any information on the Liber de Figuris and its known practitioners. It was a long shot.

Returning to Canovagasse as the early winter dusk descended felt like walking into a mouth. The house loomed, its windows dark. Klara let me in. She had not seen Emil since breakfast.

“He is in the study. He had a tray brought up at noon. He did not answer when I knocked.”

We ate a silent dinner in the cold dining room. The food was flavourless. The house felt like a held breath. Afterward, I retreated to my room. I prepared my small kit: a vial of crushed chalk and iron filings (an old trick for revealing energy flows, mostly nonsense, but sometimes the placebo of action is necessary), a silver-backed hand mirror from my valise (a risk, but a tool can be a weapon), and a stiletto-like glazier’s diamond I had pocketed from the cellar workshop.

I waited until the deep watch of the night, past two. The house was a tomb. I opened my door and slipped into the hall. This time, I did not go to the attic stairs. I went to the room next to the study—a neglected storage room, Klara had said, filled with old furniture.

The door was unlocked. Inside, it was a jungle of sheet-draped shapes under a high, small window. The air was thick with dust. I moved to the shared wall with the study. Pressing my ear to the plaster, I heard it again: the low, grinding hum. Fainter here, muffled.

I had to see. The wall was solid. But the ceiling… The house was old, with gaps in the floorboards above the lath and plaster. I dragged a heavy chest of drawers beneath a section of the ceiling, climbed onto it, and gently, with the glazier’s diamond, began to scrape away at the crumbling plaster between two beams. It was slow, nerve-wracking work. Dust sifted down into my hair, my eyes. After twenty minutes, I had made a hole the size of a coin. A sliver of green light from the study below cut through the darkness.

I put my eye to the opening.

The view was from a high corner. Emil stood below, but he was not drawing. He had cleared the centre of the room. On the floor, he had laid one of the new mirrors from the cellar—the one with the smoky, obsidian glass. It was about a meter square. Around it, he had drawn one of the geometric patterns directly onto the floorboards in what looked like black ink. This pattern was larger, more complex, interlocking with the mirror’s frame.

The hum was emanating from the mirror itself. The smoky glass was not reflecting the room. It showed a landscape, but one seen through a fever dream: jagged, crystalline structures under a bruised, non-light sky. Shapes moved there, slow and fluid, like eels in deep water.

Emil was speaking, his rustling-tomb voice resonant in the room. He was not speaking Latin, or any language I knew. It was guttural, syllabic, a language of angles and fractures. He held a small, sharp tool—an etcher’s needle. As he chanted, he knelt and began to inscribe tiny, precise versions of the geometric pattern onto the wooden frame of the mirror, connecting the larger pattern on the floor to the glass.

The bridge was not on the wall. The wall was practice. The bridge was here, on the floor, with the mirror as its gateway.

As he worked, the scene in the mirror clarified. One of the fluid shapes drew closer to the surface—our surface. It pressed against the glass from the other side. The smoky substance bulged, like a film of soap about to burst.

Emil’s chanting grew more urgent. He finished the inscription and stood back. He raised his hands, palms facing the mirror.

The glass shimmered. Then, it ceased to be a barrier. It became a surface of water, a vertical pool. The air in the study warped, the temperature plummeting. My breath fogged before my face in the cold storage room.

From the surface of the mirror, a tendril emerged. It was the colour of tarnished mercury, slick and seamless. It had no discernible texture, only a shifting, liquid solidity. It quested into the air of the study, tasting it. Then another tendril followed. They gripped the edges of the mirror’s frame.

Emil watched, his expression now one of rapturous, terrifying reverence. “Yes,” he hissed. “The anchor holds. Come. Feel this reality. Learn its weight.”

The thing in the mirror pulled. A mass began to extrude through the surface—a coalescence of the mercury-like substance, forming a rough, humanoid shape. It was headless, limbless in any human sense, a pillar of sentient fluid taking on density and presence in our world. It stood, rippling, in the centre of the pattern, connected by a umbilical cord of itself to the mirror.

Emil approached it, unafraid. He reached out a hand. The thing extended a pseudopod, which wrapped around his fingers, not in aggression, but in what seemed like… exploration. A communion.

“Soon,” Emil whispered to it. “The pattern will be complete. The bridge will be permanent. You will walk here, and I will walk there. An exchange of realms.”

My blood turned to ice. An exchange. Not an invasion. A trade. Emil wasn’t just letting something in. He was planning to go into the mirror-world. And what would step out, wearing his shape, perfected by its observations?

The thing released his hand. It turned slowly, its ‘attention’ sweeping the room. It paused. It seemed to look directly up at my spyhole.

It had no eyes. But I felt its regard, a pressure on my mind, cold and inquisitive.

Emil noticed its focus. He followed its ‘gaze’ upward, towards the corner of the ceiling where I hid.

A perfect, unpleasant smile spread across his face. “We have a guest,” he said, his voice dripping with a new, intimate malice.

I dropped from the chest, my heart hammering against my ribs. The game was up. They knew. I had to get Klara and get out. Now.

I burst from the storage room into the attic hallway. The door to the study was still shut. But as I turned to flee down the stairs, it opened.

Emil stood in the doorway, backlit by the green lamp. He held the obsidian mirror in his hands, casually, like a tray. The geometric pattern on the floor behind him was dark. The thing was gone. For now.

“Leaving so soon, Herr Varga?” he said. His voice was the pleasant, academic one again, but it was a thin veneer. The rustle was beneath it, like insects under bark. “But you’ve only just begun your investigation. Don’t you want to see how the story ends?”

I took a step backward down the stairs. “The story ends tonight, Emil. Or whatever you are.”

“What I am is the future,” he said, taking a step forward. The mirror in his hands seemed to drink the light from the hall. “A bridge between what is and what could be. You think you’re saving Klara? You’re preserving a museum. A dead world. I’m offering it life. A new kind of life.”

“You’re offering it a replacement.”

“Improvement.” He smiled. “She’ll understand. When she’s over there. It’s so much… quieter.”

He raised the mirror, not towards me, but angled it at the wall. The smoky surface caught the dim light and seemed to project the faint after-image of the geometric pattern onto the wallpaper. The air began to thicken. The low hum started again, not from the study, but from the mirror in his hands.

He was going to open a door right here, in the hallway.

I turned and ran, taking the stairs two at a time. His laughter followed me, a dry, rustling sound that seemed to come from the walls themselves.

“Run, resonance detective! Run and listen! You’ll hear us coming!”

I hit the second-floor landing and sprinted for Klara’s room. We had to get out. Now. Before the bridge in his hands was complete, and the things from the quiet, crystalline world stepped through to stay.

# Chapter 3
## CHAPTER THREE
Klara’s door was locked. I hammered on it with the flat of my hand, the sound absurdly loud in the sleeping-house silence. “Klara! Open up! Now!”
A moment of terrifying stillness, then the sound of a key turning. The door opened a crack, revealing one of her quartz eyes, wide and alert. She was still dressed.
“He knows,” I gasped, pushing into the room. It was a twin of mine, but colder, barer. “He’s not just drawing doors. He has a mirror. A portal. He’s in the hall.”
She didn’t ask for explanation. She moved with a swift, decisive energy, pulling a heavy travelling coat from a wardrobe and a small, locked metal box from under the bed. “The front door is too far. He will be on the stairs. The service entrance. Through the kitchen.”
We fled her room and down the back staircase, a tight, twisting spiral of wood that groaned under our weight. The kitchen was a cavern of looming, dark shapes—a great cast-iron range, a massive table. The

Chapter 4

CHAPTER FOUR

The rain in the other world did not fall at all. It hung. It was a suspended, crystalline mist, a billion droplets arrested in their descent, each one a tiny, perfect lens. They refracted the sourceless, opalescent light into splinters of non-color—shades that were not grey, not violet, not green, but the memory of color seen through thick ice. The air was cold and thin, like the air at a high altitude, and it carried a scent of ozone and crushed stone. It did not smell of life. It smelled of mineral patience.

We had fallen through the service entrance into a different geometry.

One moment, my hand was on the brass knob of the door leading to the Graf’s rear courtyard. The next, the substance of the door—its wood, its iron fittings, its very idea—had unraveled into a shimmering, vertical pool of mercury. There was no transition, no sense of movement. It was a cut in the film of reality. Klara, a step ahead of me, simply ceased to be on one side and was on the other. I followed, and the silence closed over my head like water.

I turned. The mercury pool was gone. In its place stood a formation of basalt pillars, hexagonal and perfectly regular, rising into the hanging mist. Our door had been deleted. The script of this place had been rewritten, and the paragraph that contained our exit had been neatly scissored out.

Klara stood a few feet away, her coat pulled tight. She was not looking at the absence of our door. She was looking at the city.

Vienna was here. And it was not.

The skeletal outlines of buildings rose in the distance, their silhouettes familiar—the Gothic spike of the Stephansdom, the neo-classical bulk of the Parliament. But they were not made of stone or brick. They were geometries of crystal and obsidian, grown rather than built, their surfaces glinting with internal fractures. The streets were not paved; they were seams of a darker, glassy material winding between the structures. And nothing moved. There were no carts, no figures, no flutter of a curtain in a non-existent window. The only sound was a low, tectonic hum, the note I had heard in the Graf’s study, now deepened into the bedrock of this world. It was the sound of a held breath.

“The quiet place,” I said. My voice was a foreign object here, absorbed almost instantly by the thick air.

Klara nodded, her quartz eyes scanning the impossible skyline. “He did not just open a door to it. He translated a piece of our world into its terms. An annex.” She hefted the metal box in her hand. “We are not in his mirror. We are in its reflection.”

I found things not meant to be found. This, I understood with a cold, professional clarity, was the ultimate iteration of my work. I had found an entire world that had been meticulously, artistically misplaced.

“We need to move,” I said. The instinct was primal. To stand still in a foreign landscape is to become a feature of it. “He will come through after us. Or his… improvements will.”

“They are already here,” Klara said softly. She pointed.

At the base of the nearest crystal structure, where a café might have been, a shape was coalescing from the mist. It resolved into the form of a man, or the memory of one. Its outline was correct—overcoat, hat—but its substance was the same smoky quartz as the buildings. It was static for a moment, a statue. Then, with a jerky, marionette smoothness, it turned its head towards us. There were no features on its face, only a smooth, polished plane that caught and bent the light.

It began to walk. Its footsteps made no sound on the glassy street.

We ran.

The rules of the landscape were a silent, brutal teacher. My first mistake was assuming it had a geography that could be memorized.

We fled down the crystalline seam of a street that should have been the Wipplingerstrasse. The quartz-man did not pursue with haste; it maintained a steady, unwavering pace, a constant in our peripheral vision. I aimed for the ghost of the Danube Canal, thinking water, a barrier, a change in terrain. But the city refused to hold its shape. A familiar corner would approach, and as we reached it, the street would fork into two impossible avenues that curved away from each other in defiance of perspective. A courtyard we ducked into would have only three walls, the fourth opening into a sheer cliff of amethyst geode. Space was not fixed here. It was suggestive. It complied with local, aesthetic rules we did not understand.

My second mistake was thinking I could hide.

We slipped into an alcove that mimicked the entrance to the Michaelerkirche. I pressed against the cold, flawless surface, willing my breathing to slow. Klara stood rigid, listening not with her ears but with her whole body. The hum of the place was a pressure on the skin.

“It does not track by sight or sound,” she whispered, her voice barely a vibration. “It tracks resonance. The discordance of our life. Our… biological noise.”

The quartz-man appeared at the head of the alley. It did not look left or right. It turned its blank face directly toward our hiding place and continued its advance.

We broke cover, and I made my third, most fundamental mistake. I tried to fight the logic of the place.

As we passed a spire that echoed the Votivkirche, I saw a loose, jagged shard of light-crystal on the ground. A weapon. A primitive impulse. I snatched it up, and when our pursuer rounded the spire, I did not throw it. I swung it like a club at the smooth plane of its face.

The shard passed through the figure as if through smoke. There was no impact, only a sudden, violent dissonance—a shriek of wrongness, like glass dragged over slate, that originated inside my own skull. The crystal in my hand vibrated, heated, and then sang in harmony with the low world-hum. The figure did not stagger. It raised a hand, and the shard dissolved from my grip into a shower of iridescent dust.

The sound had cost me. My head rang with a profound nausea. I stumbled, and the world tilted. The crystal streets seemed to pulse in time with the pain behind my eyes.

A strong, cold hand closed around my forearm. Klara hauled me upright. “Do not introduce violence,” she said, her voice sharp. “It is a language here. You just shouted a curse in a silent temple.”

She pulled me down a side seam that felt narrower, darker. The quartz-man followed, unwavering. We were tiring. It was not. The false Vienna stretched on, a beautiful, suffocating labyrinth.

“We cannot outrun it,” I gasped. “It knows the grammar of this place. We are spelling errors.”

Klara’s eyes darted, calculating. “Then we must find an editor.”

She changed our course, no longer fleeing blindly, but seeking. Her head was cocked, not listening, but feeling. She was reading the texture of the silence. She led us through a plaza that was a forest of slender, topaz obelisks, towards a structure that was a distorted echo of the Natural History Museum. But instead of grand stairs, it had a deep, dark entrance like a mine shaft.

“In here,” she said. “The hum is different. Softer. A counterpoint.”

It was a guess. A desperate, resonant guess. We plunged into the darkness.

The interior was not dark. It was full of a soft, bioluminescent glow emanating from veins in the walls. We were in a vast, subterranean gallery. But instead of dinosaur bones and meteorites, the shelves and pedestals held stillness.

Figures stood in orderly rows, hundreds of them. They were the quartz-men and women, in every variety of cut and clarity—smoky, rose, milky, clear as water. They were not moving. They were in perfect, silent repose, their blank faces all turned towards some central point we could not see. A museum of souls. A gallery of finished improvements.

At the heart of this forest of statues, a figure moved.

It was a man, or had been. He was older, his form less perfectly translated. Patches of his substance were the dull, rough texture of pumice, and a crack, fine as a hairline fracture, ran from his temple to his jaw. He wore the remnants of a curator’s coat, its cloth now fused with his crystalline skin. He was not arranging the statues. He was dusting them with a tool that emitted a soft, violet light. Where the light passed, the statues seemed to gleam with a deeper inner peace.

He saw us and froze. His eyes were not blank. They were chips of flawed emerald, and in them I saw not menace, but a terrified, scholarly caution.

Klara stepped forward, her hands open at her sides. She did not speak. She hummed. A single, pure note, low and steady. It was not the world-hum. It was a minor third below it, a gentle dissonance.

The cracked curator flinched as if struck by a loud noise. His emerald eyes widened. He raised a hand, not in threat, but in a plea for quiet. He scurried forward, his movement a series of small, precise clicks.

“You are loud,” he hissed, his voice the sound of two stones grinding softly together. “You are chaos. You will draw the Attendants.”

“We are pursued by one,” Klara said, her voice modulated to a whisper that almost matched his texture.

“Then you are already dead. You just haven’t stopped moving yet.” He looked past us, towards the entrance, his cracked face anxious. “Why have you come here? This is the Archive of Acquiescence. This is peace.”

“We seek a way back,” I said, forcing my voice into a lower register, trying to mimic the local cadence. It came out rough, gravelly.

The curator looked at me with a mixture of pity and disdain. “Back is a direction that no longer exists. You were translated. The original page is lost. You are a footnote here now.” He gestured to the silent ranks. “This is the only progression. First, the Quieting. Then, the Clarifying. Then, the Stillness. It is a beautiful logic.”

“We do not wish to be clarified,” Klara said.

“It is not a wish,” the curator replied, as if explaining rain to a child. “It is an inevitability. The resonance of your world is a scream. Here, it is smoothed. Polished. Made perfect. The Graf is a great artist. He does not destroy. He… refines.” He said the name with a reverence that was also a fear.

An idea, cold and sharp, formed in the resonant ache of my mind. “You know him. You remember before.”

A spasm of something like pain crossed the curator’s fractured face. “I remember noise. I remember… fear. It was ugly. He saved me from it.” He touched the crack on his cheek. “The process is not always flawless. Some flaws are… persistent. But the quiet is worth it.”

He was our ally. Not out of compassion, but out of a shared, desperate interest in silence. Our biological noise threatened his fragile peace. Helping us cease to be a disturbance was his only goal.

“The Attendant outside,” I pressed. “It follows our discordance. Can it be… misdirected?”

The curator’s emerald eyes glinted. He understood utility. “A larger dissonance would draw it. Overwhelm your signature.” He looked around his gallery, a possessive fear in his gaze. “Not here. You would shatter my Acquiescents. The Old Quarter. Where the translation is incomplete. Where the memories of your world still… leak. It is loud there. Painfully loud. The Attendants rarely go. It hurts them.”

“How do we get there?” Klara asked.

He gave us directions that were not directions. They were a sequence of resonant choices. Follow the hum until it sharpens to a whine. Take the vein that thrums with a blue regret. Avoid the corridors that smell of static lavender. It was a map of sensations, of pains.

“And if we find a way back?” I asked.

For the first time, a true emotion broke through his polished demeanor: a profound, weary sorrow. “There is no back. There is only deeper in. But if you find a louder place than the Old Quarter… you may delay the polishing. For a time.”

He turned his back on us, returning to his dusting, his violet light stroking the cheek of a perfectly still quartz-child. Our audience was over. We were an unpleasant ripple in his still pond, already fading.

We moved deeper into the archive, following the curator’s sensory map. As we left the glowing hall, I looked back. He was a lonely, cracked figure tending his garden of silence, a prisoner who loved his bars because they kept the terrifying music of life at bay.

The Old Quarter was a wound in the side of the quiet world.

The transition was abrupt. One moment, the crystal seams were smooth, the light opalescent and even. The next, the walls became a sickly hybrid of crumbling brick and protruding citrine. The air grew thick and warm, carrying a scent we knew too well: the damp stone, the distant grease-paint, the ghost of coffee. Vienna’s memory, bleeding through.

And the sound. It was a cacophony of echoes without source. The faint clatter of a phantom tram, the murmur of a crowd that wasn’t there, a distant strain of a waltz played on a warped violin. It was not loud to our ears, but to the body. It set my teeth on edge. My skin prickled as if with static. Klara winced with every step, her quartz eyes dimming slightly, as if trying to shut down.

“The discordance,” she said, her voice strained. “It is not clean. It is a sickness here.”

We had our misdirection. But the Attendant, as the curator predicted, did not follow us in. It stopped at the precise border where the pure crystal became infected memory. It stood sentinel, a smooth, silent guardian against the noise. We were trapped in a quarantine zone of our own world’s ghost.

The weakness it revealed in me was not physical. It was existential. Klara, with her engineered nature, could parse the noise. She could separate the resonant layers, treat it as a complex but readable signal. For me, it was just pain. It was the death rattle of everything I knew—its architecture, its rhythms, its rain—reduced to a maddening, persistent echo. It eroded my focus. It made me doubt the solidity of my own thoughts. I was a man made of noise, dissolving in a sea of louder, angrier noise.

We found a niche that was half a burgher’s house fireplace, half a geode. I slumped against it, pressing the heels of my hands into my eyes. “We cannot stay here. This will unmake us before the Attendants do.”

Klara was examining the wall, her fingers tracing the seam where brick met crystal. “It is a boundary. A limit of the translation. The Graf’s power—or his mirror’s power—is not absolute here. The original substrate is too strong. Too… sentimental.”

“Sentiment doesn’t make a door,” I said, the ghost of a waltz twisting in my gut.

“No,” she agreed. “But a flaw in the polish might make a crack.”

She opened the metal box she had carried from her room. Inside, nestled in felt, were not tools, but objects: a tarnished silver thimble, a fragment of a ceramic doll’s face, a single pearl earring, a lock of hair tied with a faded ribbon. The detritus of a lost life. Her life.

“Resonance is not just sound,” she said, selecting the pearl earring. “It is memory. It is attachment. This place,” she gestured to the weeping brick, “is rich with it. Unwanted. Unrefined.” She pressed the pearl against the brick. “We must offer it a memory of our own. One strong enough to make a sympathetic vibration. A flaw it recognizes.”

I had nothing like that. My pockets held only practical things: a folding knife, a few coins, the key to my rooms in the real Vienna, now meaningless. My memories were not keepsakes. They were the rain, the coffee steam, the weight of a lost object in my hand. They were verbs, not nouns.

Klara closed her eyes, holding the earring to the wall. She began to hum again, but this time it was a melody. A simple, sad folk tune. A lullaby. As she hummed, the pearl began to glow with a soft, milky light. The brick around it darkened, becoming more real, more solid. The crystal retreated a fraction.

But it was not enough. The wall held. Her single memory was a whisper against the roar of the quarter’s pain.

My weakness was my humanity. My noise. But noise, the curator said, was what this place remembered. What it was made of.

I did not have an object. I had a sense. A profession. I found things. I stood up, placing my hand flat on the wall next to Klara’s earring. I did not hum. I did the only thing I could think to do. I described. In a low, steady voice, pushing against the phantom waltz, I painted a picture with the only paints I had.

“The rain on the black wool of my overcoat,” I began, my voice rough. “The way it beads, like tiny mercury planets. The smell of wet stone and chestnut blossoms from the stall on the corner. The sound of a particular heel-click on wet cobblestones at 3 a.m., a sound that means someone is going home, or not going home. The weight of a lost wedding ring in my palm, still warm from the hand it left. The taste of whipped cream and bitterness in a small cup, while waiting for a ghost.”

I was not offering a memory. I was offering a method. A way of being. A way of listening to the world’s discordant, beautiful noise and calling it a map.

The wall beneath my hand grew warm. The brick texture spread, swallowing more of the crystal. A hairline fracture appeared in the mortar, glowing with a faint, golden light. Not the cold light of this place, but the warm, smoky light of a gas lamp.

Klara’s lullaby and my catalogue of losses intertwined. The pearl’s glow brightened. The crack widened, with a sound like distant ice breaking on the Danube.

It was not a door. It was a fissure. A jagged, vertical split in reality, about the width of a man’s body. Through it, I saw not the Graf’s hallway, but a misty, pre-dawn street. The real street. I could smell the real rain.

“Go,” Klara said, her voice taut with effort. The fissure was unstable, flickering.

I did not argue. I squeezed through the crack. The transition was violent, a roar of returning sound—real sound, of dripping water, a distant dog, the rustle of wind in real leaves. I stumbled onto the slick cobbles of a narrow alley I recognized, somewhere in the Spittelberg district. The air was gloriously, filthily alive.

I turned to help Klara through.

The fissure shimmered. From within the quartz world, a smooth, grey hand clamped onto Klara’s shoulder. An Attendant, braving the edge of the noise. It was pulling her back.

Her quartz eyes met mine. There was no fear in them. Only a fierce, calculated resolve. With her free hand, she slammed the metal box containing her memories into my chest.

“Find the source, Leo,” she said. “The mirror is not the source. Find the silence.”

Then she did not fight the pull. She turned into it, driving her elbow back into the Attendant’s form. The two of them vanished into the glowing fracture, which collapsed in on itself with a soundless, final snap.

I was alone on the wet cobbles. The box was cold in my hands. The false dawn was a smear of grey in the east. The small victory of escape was ashes. I had forged an alliance and watched it be swallowed by the quiet. I had used my weakness as a key, only to have the lock close on the one person who understood the lock.

The Graf’s words echoed in the real, beautiful, noisy rain. You’ll hear us coming.

Klara was gone. And I was left with the one thing I had always been best at finding: a profound, echoing, and utterly necessary loss. I opened the box. The pearl earring, its glow extinguished, lay beside the doll’s fragment, the ribbon, the thimble. A museum of a person.

I closed the lid. The rain beaded on the black wool of my overcoat. I had a new thing to find. Not a ghost this time.

I had to find the silence before it found everyone else.

Chapter 5

CHAPTER FIVE

The silence Klara had spoken of was not an absence. I understood that now, standing in the rain that was only rain. It was a presence. A thing with edges and intention. It was the future the past had been building, brick by quiet brick, and it was coming for the noisy, messy, unbearable present. I had spent my life finding lost things. Now I was to find a lost world, one that had not yet happened, but was already reaching back to erase the steps that led to it. The paradox was a cold stone in my gut, heavier than the metal box in my pocket.

I walked. The Spittelberg alley gave way to a wider street, then to the grand, sleeping mouth of the Mariahilfer Strasse. The city was stirring in its grey cocoon. A tram clattered by, its bell a sharp, metallic life. A shopkeeper winched an awning with a ratcheting shriek that was music. I drank the noise like a man dying of thirst. Each sound was a stitch holding the fabric of this world together. Klara had traded herself for a principle: that a world with a dog barking in the distance was worth saving from a world of perfect, polished peace.

My destination was not my own sparse rooms. They were a listening post, not an arsenal. For what I needed, there was only one place. The past had to arm me for the future.

Scene 1

The Antiquariat des Unmöglichen occupied a wedge of crumbling stone where two streets in the Alsergrund district disagreed on their angle. Its windows were perpetually dusty, its doorbell a rusted shriek that had, decades ago, given up and fallen silent. It did not deal in first editions or handsome maps. It dealt in errors. In misprints of Bibles that suggested blasphemous new commandments. In maps where coastlines bled into speculative, nightmare continents. In musical scores annotated by composers in the grip of fevers, the notes forming sigils that made the eyes water. Its proprietor was a man named Emil, who believed all of history was a palimpsest, and his job was to find the scratches underneath the official text.

I pushed the door open. The smell was the same as always: foxed paper, beeswax, and the faint, electric tang of ozone that clung to certain unstable folios.

“The rain is unspooling particularly threadily today, Leo,” a voice said from a canyon of shelves. “It suggests a celestial hand with poor motor control.”

Emil emerged, a stooped figure in a cardigan the colour of weak tea. His eyes, magnified by thick spectacles, were the only quick thing about him. They darted over me, inventorying the damp, the tension in my shoulders, the new hollows under my eyes.

“Emil. I need a guide.”

He blinked slowly. “To where? The municipal library is two blocks east. I hear their travel section is adequate.”

“To a place that isn’t on any map. A place that’s… being prepared.”

He removed his spectacles and began polishing them on his cardigan. A stalling technique. “The future is notoriously poorly catalogued. It lacks reliable primary sources.”

“This isn’t the future. It’s a past future. A road that was built but never travelled. I’ve seen the paving stones.”

He went very still. The cloth stopped moving on the glass. “You’ve been to the quartz city.”

It was not a question. I said nothing. My silence was confirmation enough.

He replaced his spectacles, his gaze now sharp, avaricious. “Describe the acoustics.”

“There are none. It absorbs. It aims for perfection.”

“Perfection,” he scoffed, turning and shuffling towards the back room. I followed. “A tyrannical concept. The most interesting things happen in the flaws. The crack in the bell, the slip of the engraver’s hand, the wrong word in the right place.” He led me into a cluttered back room dominated by a massive, scarred oak table. “This… silence. It is an active suppression?”

“It’s a cultivation. They prune the noise. They are the gardeners of quiet. And they’re coming to prune us.”

Emil hummed, unlocking a tall, iron-strapped cabinet with a key from a chain around his neck. “The past is littered with attempts to build the future. Utopian blueprints. Philosophical frameworks. Most are harmless, paper fantasies.” He pulled out a portfolio, not of paper, but of thin, hammered copper sheets, bound with wire. “Some, however, were attempts not to design, but to excavate. To uncover a future that was already there, waiting, like a fossil in stone.”

He laid the portfolio on the table. The copper was verdigrised at the edges. He opened it. Inside, diagrams were etched not in ink, but in fine, hair-thin grooves. “The Zukunftsgeologen,” he said. “The Future-Geologists. A small, Viennese secret society. Circa 1887. Their thesis was that time is a sedimentary rock. The present is the topsoil. The past is the strata beneath. And the future… the future is the bedrock. Already formed. Already complete. They believed one could drill down, through the loose shale of possibility, to that solid, inevitable core.”

I leaned over the etchings. They were architectural, but of a bizarre kind. Cross-sections of reality. Layers labelled “Chaos-Strata,” “Probability-Schist,” and at the bottom, “Fundamentum Certitudinis”—the Foundation of Certainty.

“They were mystics,” I said.

“They were miners,” Emil corrected. “They sought tools. Sonic drills to shatter the layers of chance. Tuning forks to resonate with the bedrock. They believed if you could achieve perfect structural harmony with the true, coming world, you would be… preserved. Like a insect in amber, while the chaotic, transitional world around you crumbled to dust.”

A cold understanding crystallized. “The quartz city. It’s not a design. It’s an excavation. They’ve drilled down to their bedrock future. And now they’re smoothing away the imperfect strata above it. Us.”

Emil nodded, tapping a specific diagram. It showed a device like a complex sextant crossed with a phonograph horn. “Their primary tool. A ‘Harmonic Diviner.’ It doesn’t predict the future. It attunes to it. It finds the frequency of the Fundamentum and begins to resonate. That resonance… it would, over time, calibrate all malleable matter around it to the same perfect pitch. Starting with sound. Then light. Then thought.”

“Where is it?” The words were gravel in my throat.

“According to their last, fragmented minutes, they believed the bedrock future was most accessible at a point of concentrated historical decision. A place where many possible paths had once existed, but only one was taken. A nexus of closed doors.” He looked at me over his spectacles. “The Hofburg. Specifically, the Reichskanzleitrakt. The Imperial Chancellery wing. Where an empire was governed, and where it was signed out of existence.”

The heart of old Vienna. The seat of the noise they so despised.

“They built it there? In the palace?”

“They began,” Emil said. “The records stop. Either they succeeded and entered their bedrock, leaving no trace… or they drilled into something they couldn’t control. A fault line. An aquifer of pure, howling possibility that drowned them.” He closed the copper portfolio with a soft clap. “You are not looking for a machine, Leo. You are looking for a hole. A borehole into tomorrow. And you must decide whether to seal it… or to follow Klara down.”

He was giving me the guide. Not a map, but a theory. A past madness that explained the future’s cold architecture. It was all I had.

“I need the minutes. The fragments.”

He handed me the portfolio. “It is on loan. I expect its return. Preferably without the scent of apocalypse on it.”

As I took the heavy sheets, my hand brushed against the metal box in my pocket. A museum of a person. I had one more question. “The Zukunftsgeologen… did they have symbols? Attendants?”

Emil thought. “They were scholars. Not soldiers. But their ideal… a smoothed, perfect, silent world… it would need custodians. Enforcers of the quiet. They called them, in one draft, ‘Die Glätter.’ The Smoothers.”

The Attendants. The name fit them like a grey glove.

I left the shop with the copper portfolio under my arm. The rain had thinned to a mist. The city’s sounds seemed sharper now, more fragile. Each one a defiance against the smoothing. I had my resource. A theory of the fracture in the world. Now I needed a team to walk into it.

Scene 2

The journey to the Hofburg was not measured in streets, but in layers of time. I walked from the scholarly gloom of the Alsergrund, across the Ringstrasse, and into the Imperial precinct. The weight of the past here was not melancholic; it was administrative. It was the weight of ledgers and decrees, of portraits of dead Habsburgs with eyes like dull coins. The buildings were vast, correct, and empty of the life that had once animated them. It was a perfect shell. I felt the irony like a needle-prick. The Smoothers would appreciate this place. It was already halfway to quartz.

I did not go to the public squares. I moved through the service arteries, the cobbled back ways where once coal was delivered and secrets were taken out with the trash. My team was not one I would have chosen. It was one that had chosen me, through the simple, brutal logic of shared exposure.

I found Tomas in a tiny, steam-clouded Konditorei a stone’s throw from the Burgtheater. He was hunched over a plate of Gugelhupf, not eating it, just staring as if the cake’s swirls held a code. The big man looked diminished, his shoulders rounded inward. The memory of the phantom music, the memory of his own hands trying to still his own heart, had left a film on him.

I sat opposite him. The copper portfolio lay on the table between us.

“I know where the silence is coming from,” I said, without preamble.

He didn’t look up. “Good for you.”

“Klara is inside it. She traded herself for my escape.”

That made his eyes lift. They were red-rimmed. “Why?”

“Because she believes the noise is worth saving. The mess. The pain. The wrong notes.” I leaned forward. “I’m going to find the machine making the quiet. I’m going to break it. And I’m going to try to pull her out. I could use a man who understands that sometimes, you have to fight the music.”

A long silence. The hiss of the coffee machine was a tiny, brave rebellion. Tomas placed his huge hands flat on the table. “My violin is ash. My hands… they remember the wrong things.”

“Then bring your hands. And your anger. It’s a noisy thing. They’ll hate it.”

He gave a single, slow nod. “Where?”

“The Hofburg. The old Chancellery.”

A ghost of his old, cynical smile touched his lips. “Of course. The quiet men always want the big, empty houses.”

Next was the Graf. He was not hiding. He was holding court. I found him in a private room at the back of a grand café near the Opera, surrounded by three young, intense men in suits that were too new. He was speaking in a low, urgent monotone about “acoustic sovereignty” and “sonic hygiene.” He saw me in the doorway and froze mid-sentence.

“Gentlemen, a moment,” he said, his voice regaining its oil. He rose and came to the door, steering me into the hushed, marble hallway. “Herr Varga. You are… damp. And you have the look of a man who has seen the blueprint of his own obsolescence.”

“You told me I’d hear you coming,” I said. “I’m going to the source. You can hear it with me, or you can stay here and recruit foot soldiers for a war that will be over before you muster them. Your choice.”

His eyes narrowed. The fanatic in him warred with the pragmatist. The pragmatist won. “The source? You found it?”

“The Zukunftsgeologen. They drilled a hole. Your ‘Smoothers’ are the janitors of that hole. I’m going to plug it.”

He absorbed this, his mind racing behind his cool eyes. To be there, at the genesis of the quiet… for him, it was like being offered a pilgrimage to Mecca. “What do you need?”

“Your ears. Your understanding of what they’re building. And your… persuasive abilities. If we meet resistance.”

“You want me to fight my own ideal?”

“I want you to see its birth canal,” I said. “Decide if you like the midwives.”

He glanced back at his acolytes, then at me. The decision was made. “When?”

“Now.”

Our procession to the Hofburg was a silent, grim thing. Tomas, a mountain of bruised defiance. The Graf, a coiled spring of conflicted zeal. And me, a finder of lost things, carrying a box of lost memories and a map to a lost tomorrow. We were not an army. We were a biopsy needle, aiming for the tumour’s core.

The Chancellery wing loomed ahead, a long, severe façade of grey stone. It was here, in 1918, that the last Habsburg emperor had signed the renunciation of his powers. A world had ended with the scratch of a pen. Perhaps the Zukunftsgeologen thought that scratch was the first chip in the bedrock.

Scene 3

We stood in the shadow of a massive archway leading to a deserted inner courtyard. The rain had stopped, leaving the flagstones slick and black. The only sound was the distant, rhythmic click of a guard’s boots on stone, fading away on his rounds. The silence here was different from the quartz city. It was old, exhausted, the silence of abandoned power. But underneath it, I could feel a hum. Not a sound, but a vibration in the teeth, a pressure against the eardrums. The Diviner was here. It was active.

Tomas rubbed his sternum, his face pale. “I can feel it. In my bones. It’s… tuning me.”

The Graf inhaled deeply, his eyes closed in a kind of rapture. “The purity of it. It is not an absence. It is a… potential. A vacuum waiting to be filled with perfect order.”

“It’s a drain,” I said, opening the copper portfolio under the dim light of a security lamp. The etched diagrams shimmered. “They picked this spot because it’s a sinkhole of historical energy. They’re not broadcasting the future. They’re sucking the present down into it.” I pointed to a schematic. “The Diviner would need a stable, foundational chamber. Below ground level. Protected from ‘acoustic interference’—from life.”

“The cellars,” the Graf murmured. “The imperial archives have sub-levels. Forgotten. Forbidden.”

Tensions surfaced like cracks in ice. Tomas glared at the Graf. “You sound like you want to find it just to kneel in front of it.”

The Graf’s gaze was cool. “I want to understand it. To know if the price of perfection is worth paying. You, musician, you would pay with your art. What is that to the man who has no art? Only noise and pain?”

“My art is noise and pain!” Tomas growled, his voice too loud in the courtyard. It echoed off the stone, a beautiful, messy reverberation that made us all wince. For a moment, the hum in our teeth seemed to flinch. “It is feeling! Your perfect world has no feeling. It is a museum, and you are the dust on the floor.”

“Enough,” I said, my voice low. The argument was a distraction, but it was also a proof. We were still messy, still flawed, still capable of discord. We were still alive. “We find the entrance. The records suggest a service stair, behind a ‘door of false records.’ A filing room for discontinued ministries.”

The search was tense, silent but for our breathing and the scuff of our shoes. The grandeur of the halls gave way to narrower, plainer corridors. We found the room: a bleak, windowless space lined with wooden filing cabinets that smelled of mildew and oblivion. According to Emil’s notes, the cabinet marked “K.u.K. Zukunftsangelegenheiten” (Imperial and Royal Future Affairs) was the trigger. A joke, or a perfect hiding place.

I pulled the cabinet. It was not on rollers. It swung outwards on a silent, well-oiled pivot, revealing a spiral staircase of black iron descending into darkness.

The hum was stronger here. A sub-audible thrum that made the fillings in my teeth ache. The air from below was cool, dry, and carried a scent that was unmistakable: ozone, and beneath it, the faint, clean smell of polished stone.

The Graf pushed forward, eager. Tomas hung back, his fear a tangible thing.

“This is it,” the Graf whispered. “The borehole.”

I took the small flashlight from my pocket. Its beam was a pathetic, yellow intrusion into the perfect black. “Stay close. And stay noisy. Argue. Breathe heavily. Scuff your feet. Your imperfection is your armour here.”

We descended. The iron stairs seemed to go on forever, coiling down through the foundations of the empire. The hum grew into a pressure, a physical weight on the skin. The walls changed from brick to naked bedrock, and then, to something else. They began to show a faint, internal luminescence. A familiar, milky glow.

Quartz. We were entering the vein.

Scene 4

The staircase ended. We stood at the mouth of a cavern that should not have existed beneath Vienna. It was not a natural formation. It was a geode, grown around the seed of the machine. The walls, floor, and high ceiling were all smooth, polished quartz, glowing with that same sourceless, shadowless light. It was the quartz city in miniature, a perfect, sealed cyst.

And in the centre of the cavern stood the Harmonic Diviner.

It was both less and more than I had imagined. It was not a towering engine. It was an elegant, disturbing sculpture of brass, glass, and what looked like solidified mercury. A central plinth of black basalt supported a complex armature of rotating rings, each inscribed with symbols that were not letters, but representations of sonic waveforms frozen in metal. At its heart, suspended in a glass sphere, was a core of vibrating, liquid silver. It did not hum. It was the hum. The pressure, the ache, the sense of being un-tuned radiated from it in visible waves—distortions in the air like heat haze.

Around the machine, in a circle, stood six figures. Not Attendants. These were different. They wore dark, old-fashioned suits, their faces pale and still. They did not move. They were not statues. They were men. Preserved. Petrified not in stone, but in the absolute certainty of the future they had drilled into. The Zukunftsgeologen. They had found their bedrock. They had become part of the foundation.

But they were not alone. Between them and us, standing sentinel, were three Attendants. Their smooth, grey faces turned towards us in unison. There was no hostility in their gaze. Only a profound, corrective intent. We were the flaw in the crystal. The noise in the signal.

We stood at the entrance, our ragged breathing an obscenity in the tuned air. This was the location of the central ordeal. Not a stronghold, but a shrine. Not a heart of darkness, but a heart of terrible, sterile light.

Tomas let out a choked sound. The Graf simply stared, his face a mask of awe and terror.

I felt the metal box in my pocket, cold against my thigh. Klara’s museum. Her memories were of a noisy, painful, vibrant life. This place was the antithesis of all of them. This was the silence she had told me to find. The source.

One of the Attendants took a smooth step forward. It raised a hand, not in a threat, but in a gesture of… offering. An invitation to be still. To be smoothed. To join the perfect, silent future.

The Graf took a half-step forward. I saw the temptation in him. The desire to end the struggle, to submit to the order.

Tomas began to shake. Not with fear, but with a building, violent resonance. He was fighting the tuning. His hands clenched into fists.

I looked past the Attendant, to the machine. To the liquid silver core. The source of the silence. Klara’s words were the only noise in my head.

Find the silence.

I had found it. Now I had to decide what to do with it. Destroy it? A part of me wanted to, violently. To smash the glass sphere and let the screaming chaos of the world flood this sacred, hateful space.

But another part, the finder, knew that was not the way. You did not destroy a lost thing. You understood it. You followed its thread back to where it belonged.

The Attendant was almost upon us. The Graf was trembling on the brink. Tomas was a coiled spring of discordant humanity.

I reached into my pocket and closed my hand not around the box, but around the single object I had placed there from it this morning. The pearl earring. Its glow was extinguished, but it was a piece of her. A piece of the noisy, imperfect past.

I held it up. The flawless pearl looked dull against the radiant quartz.

“This is a memory,” I said, my voice a rough, human scrape in the tuned air. The Attendant paused. “It is of a woman who chose the noise. Who fought for the right to be flawed. You want the future? It is built of this. Not of silence. Of choice.”

I didn’t throw it at the machine. I dropped it. It fell to the quartz floor with a tiny, definitive click.

The sound was minuscule. A grain of sand in a desert of quiet.

But in that perfect chamber, attuned to eliminate all discord, the tiny, random, meaningless click of a pearl hitting stone was an atom bomb.

The Attendant flinched. The liquid silver in the machine’s core shivered, its perfect vibration stuttering. The hum fractured, just for a microsecond, into a discordant ring.

And in that ring, I heard something else. A whisper. Not from the machine.

From one of the petrified Zukunftsgeologen.

His lips had not moved. His stone eyes had not shifted. But the sound had found him, in his bedrock certainty. And from within his perfect preservation came a faint, despairing, human sigh.

The future was not perfect. It was a tomb. And its occupants were still, terribly, alive inside it.

The Attendant’s smooth face showed its first ripple of something like confusion. The Graf gasped, his dream shattering. Tomas roared, a raw, unfiltered, gloriously noisy sound of defiance, and charged.

The moment of reflection was over. The ordeal had begun. We had entered the silence. And we had brought the storm with us.

Chapter 6

CHAPTER SIX

The silence did not shatter. It curdled.

The Attendant’s hesitation was a geological event, a continental plate shifting behind a face of polished bone. Tomas’s roar was the quake. It hit the chamber not as a wave of sound, but as a physical distortion, a ripple in the substance of the air itself. The tuned perfection of the space recoiled. The hum of the machine, that single, pure note meant to shepherd time into a clean line, splintered into a chord. And in that chord were all the notes it had been built to exclude: the rasp of desperation, the thud of a heartbeat, the wet crack of a sob held too long.

Tomas was not a large man, but his charge was tectonic. He did not move toward the Attendant, but toward the machine, the source of the frequency. His body was a projectile of glorious, useless defiance. The Attendant flowed to intercept, a liquid shadow resolving into solid form. They met not with a clash, but with a terrible, absorbing thud, like a stone dropped into deep mud. Tomas’s momentum died in that embrace. The Attendant’s hands, smooth and cool as river stones, closed around his shoulders. There was no violence in the motion. It was a correction. A silencing.

I saw Tomas’s face over the Attendant’s shoulder. His eyes were wide, not with fear, but with a furious, final understanding. He had been a discordant note his whole life. Here, in the temple of perfect pitch, he would be resolved.

“No!” The word was torn from the Graf, a thread of his old, passionate self pulled through the tapestry of his new dogma. He took a halting step forward, his hand outstretched not toward Tomas, but toward the machine, as if to steady its song.

I did not move. My eyes were on the pearl earring where it had come to rest, a milwhite apostrophe against the glowing quartz. The tiny click of its fall still reverberated in the bones of my skull. It was not an echo. It was a key, turning in a lock I had not known was there.

The sigh from the petrified geologist had done it. It had not been a sound of the lungs, but of the soul—a leak in the perfect seal of the future. And it had whispered a single, devastating truth: this preservation was not peace. It was a conscious, unending internment. They were not specimens. They were witnesses, trapped behind their own eyes, watching eternity stream past in a single, unbearable note.

The Attendant held Tomas, immobile. Its head tilted, not at the struggling man, but at the machine. The liquid silver core was shivering, its vibration now imperfect, a master violinist with a hairline crack in their instrument. The pearl, a piece of noisy, sentimental past, had introduced a flaw. A memory had become a virus.

“The frequency is compromised,” the Attendant stated. Its voice was still a neutral bell, but the toll was now faintly uncertain. “Anomaly introduced. Initiating purification.”

Its gaze—a direction of attention more than an act of seeing—swung to me. Then to the pearl. The calculus was simple. Remove the anomaly.

It released Tomas. He slumped to the floor, not unconscious, but emptied, as if the Attendant had siphoned the very noise from his cells. The being took a step toward the earring. Its intention was clear: to dissolve the irregularity, to re-tune the chamber back to its pristine state.

This was the confrontation. Not with fists or weapons, but with ontology. The Attendant was the embodied principle of a chosen future. I was a relic of a discarded past, holding a fragment of a woman who had laughed too loud and loved too messily. My greatest fear had never been death. It had been irrelevance. To be a footnote in a story that had already chosen its ending. To find things only to have them erased by a future that considered them stains.

And here, in this gleaming tomb for time, I was facing exactly that. Erasure.

I moved. Not to attack, but to kneel. My knees hit the quartz with a jarring crack that felt profoundly, satisfyingly human. My fingers closed around the pearl a moment before the Attendant’s would have. The touch was not cold. It held the phantom warmth of a earlobe, of a life.

The Attendant stopped. It looked at my closed fist, then at my face. “You are also an anomaly,” it said. “The Varga thread is knotted. Illogical. You seek the past within the future. It is a contradiction. It must be smoothed.”

“Smoothing is just another word for forgetting,” I said, my voice low. I looked past it, to the Graf. He was staring at the machine, his face a battleground. His dream of a clean, silent tomorrow was rupturing before him, and the sound of the rupture was the ragged breathing of the friend he had betrayed. “You wanted a world without ghosts, Graf. But you built a mausoleum. And ghosts aren’t the dead who haunt us. They’re the living we choose to bury.”

His eyes met mine. In them, I saw the last vestige of the man who had once shared a bottle of Zirbenschnaps with me in a dusty archive, his eyes alight with the sheer romance of a forgotten story. That man was drowning in the silver light of his perfect future.

The Attendant’s hand came toward my forehead. Not to strike. To touch. A purifying touch, I knew. It would not kill me. It would un-knot me. It would smooth my memories, my contradictions, my noisy, searching life into a calm, coherent line that led obediently to this silent, silver room. It was the ultimate failure.
